    </p><div id="art-admin"><span class="italic bold">Received 
      8th May 2024
    </span><span class="bold italic">, Accepted 1st September 2024</span></div><p class="bold italic">First published on 4th September 2024</p><hr><div class="abstract"><h2>Abstract</h2><p>As a promising anode candidate for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), tin-based oxides suffer from rapid capacity fading, greatly limiting their practical applications. Herein, we designed and synthesized three cobaltâ€“tin oxide composites (CSOs), with different degrees of crystallinity by controlling the annealing temperature, to understand the effect of amorphous and crystalline structures on the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> storage behavior of tin-based alloy anodes. Theoretical calculations suggest that the amorphous CSO (CSO-A) presents the lowest binding energy with Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> and the lowest diffusion barriers of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> in comparison with that of crystallinity samples (CSO-AC and CSO-C), indicating that the amorphous CSO is the most energetically favorable for Na insertion. Similarly, the experimental results suggest that CSO-A delivers the highest initial specific capacity; however, it presents the worst cycling stability and reversibility. CSO-C displays the best cycling stability but the lowest specific capacity. Interestingly, the CSO-AC sample with both amorphous and crystalline domains achieves the best comprehensive electrochemical performance. Quantitative analysis of the electrochemical process reveals that controlled crystallinity significantly impacts the microstructure and band gap of CSO, which will further affect the reversibility of the conversion reaction and the percent of pseudocapacitance contribution. Our work suggests that, for the alloy anode, rational regulation of crystallinity is a substantial approach to improve capacity retention.</p></div><hr>
    
      <h2 id="sect294"><span class="a_heading">Introduction</span></h2>
      <span>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are gaining greater attention for grid-scale electrochemical energy storage applications due to their low cost and sustainability compared to reputable lithium-ion batteries (LIBs). The Earth's crustal abundance of Na resources is over 1388 times that of Li, which adds to the appeal of SIBs.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1,2</span></sup></a> However, urgent attention is needed to develop high energy density, long lifespan, and low-cost SIBs. Developing new electrode materials with advanced and novel chemistries is undoubtedly fundamental for enhancing the electrochemical performance of batteries.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit3"><sup><span class="sup_ref">3</span></sup></a> Tin oxide (SnO<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>)-based nanomaterials are considered highly promising anode materials for SIBs due to their exceptionally high theoretical capacity of 1378 mA h g<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small>. The capacity is substantially greater than that of the widely used hard carbon anodes (300â€“350 mA h g<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small>).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit4"><sup><span class="sup_ref">4</span></sup></a> The superior capacity of SnO<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>-based materials is attributed to the multiple electron transfer processes involved in conversion and alloying reactions with sodium ions as follows:<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit5"><sup><span class="sup_ref">5</span></sup></a><br><table><tbody><tr><td class="eqnText">Conversion reaction: SnO<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small> + 2<span class="italic">x</span>Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> + 2<span class="italic">x</span>e<small><sup>âˆ’</sup></small> â†” Sn + <span class="italic">x</span>Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>O</td></tr></tbody></table><br><table><tbody><tr><td class="eqnText">Alloying reaction: Sn + <span class="italic">x</span>Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> + <span class="italic">x</span>e<small><sup>âˆ’</sup></small> â†” Nn<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Sn (<span class="italic">x</span> â‰¤ 3.75)</td></tr></tbody></table></span>
      <p class="otherpara">In practice, SnO<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>-based materials for SIBs face several hindrances, including undesirable capacity fading, a severe volume change with Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> ions, and the pulverization/agglomeration of primitive particles during the alloying/dealloying process.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit6"><sup><span class="sup_ref">6</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">To address the aforementioned issues, various approaches have been explored from the perspective of chemical composition<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit7"><sup><span class="sup_ref">7,8</span></sup></a> and hierarchical structure.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit9"><sup><span class="sup_ref">9â€“11</span></sup></a> One effective method involves the incorporation of active or inactive buffering components such as Co,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit7"><sup><span class="sup_ref">7</span></sup></a> P,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit8"><sup><span class="sup_ref">8</span></sup></a> and Cu<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit12"><sup><span class="sup_ref">12</span></sup></a> into tin-based oxides to improve their cycling stability. For instance, Sun <span class="italic">et al.</span> have demonstrated cobaltâ€“tin oxide (CSO) materials with a high theoretical capacity of above 1000 mA h g<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small> because the reduced metallic Co acts as a conductive matrix and prevents the aggregation of Sn particles during alloying and dealloying.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit13"><sup><span class="sup_ref">13</span></sup></a> Additionally, the hierarchical nanostructures of tin-based oxides<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit9"><sup><span class="sup_ref">9,10,14</span></sup></a> were also designed to improve the cycling stability and reversibility. However, these nanostructures not only require a substantial amount of polymeric binders and carbon additives to maintain the electrode integrity and electrical contact, but also induce serious side reactions with the electrolyte because of their high surface area.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit15"><sup><span class="sup_ref">15</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">Recently, the academic community focused on regulating the intrinsic structures of electrode materials to improve their electrochemical properties. Lu <span class="italic">et al.</span> found that phase engineering directly impacts nanomaterials' properties and functions. The amorphous phase-engineered VO<small><sub>2</sub></small> exhibits superior K<small><sup>+</sup></small> storage ability and alleviated volume variation compared to the crystalline phase VO<small><sub>2</sub></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit16"><sup><span class="sup_ref">16</span></sup></a> In addition, the researchers reported that vanadium oxide nanobelt (VO-E) integrated amorphousâ€“crystalline nanoscale domains achieved a higher discharge specific capacity of 516 mA h g<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small>, and a better rate and cycling performance for Zn<small><sup>2+</sup></small> ion storage. The unique structure of VO-E facilitates cation adsorption and lowers the ion diffusion energy.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit17"><sup><span class="sup_ref">17</span></sup></a> Moreover, it has been found that the crystallinity could affect the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> reaction kinetics, especially the pseudocapacitive storage.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit18"><sup><span class="sup_ref">18</span></sup></a> Extensive studies have focused more on revealing the relationship between crystallinity and specific capacity for intercalation electrodes. However, it is still unclear how the crystallinity affects the electrochemical properties of tin-based alloy anode materials.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">Herein, we designed and synthesized three cobaltâ€“tin oxide composite (CSO) samples with varying degrees of crystallinity, such as amorphous (CSO-A), heterogenous amorphous/crystalline (CSO-AC), crystalline (CSO-C), by controlling the annealing temperature at 400, 600, and 800 Â°C, respectively. The three samples were evaluated for their capacity as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries to uncover the effect of crystallinity on fundamental Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> storage behaviors. The results indicate that CSO-A, with the highest specific surface area, highly porous, disordered structure, and the highest band gap, delivers the highest initial reversible specific capacity but suffers from the most rapid capacity fading; CSO-C, with the lowest specific area, crystal structure, and the lowest band gap, presents the lowest initial specific capacity but superior cycling stability without capacity fading. Interestingly, CSO-AC, with a lower specific surface area, both amorphous and crystalline structure, and a lower band gap, displays a higher initial reversible specific capacity but the highest reversible specific capacity after cycling. The theoretical and experimental results suggest that appropriate crystallinity regulation can significantly enhance the electrochemical performance, such as specific capacity and cycling stability, by controlling the microstructure and band gap, which will further affect the amount of active sites, the reversibility of the conversion reaction, and the percent of pseudocapacitance contribution. This study highlights the importance of understanding and controlling crystallinity to improve capacity retention for tin oxide-based materials.</p>
    
    
      <h2 id="sect353"><span class="a_heading">Results</span></h2>
      
        <h3 id="sect355"><span class="b_heading">The morphology and microstructure of CSOs</span></h3>
        <span>The overall synthetic route of CSO samples with distinct crystallinity degrees is illustrated as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1a</a>. Firstly, the pink cobalt hydroxystannate precursor was synthesized by mixing tin(<span class="small_caps">IV</span>), cobalt(<span class="small_caps">II</span>) and hydroxide species based on a coprecipitation reaction (<a title="" href="#eqn1">reaction (1)</a>).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit19"><sup><span class="sup_ref">19</span></sup></a> After reaction, the composition of the precursor was confirmed by the X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern displayed in Fig. S1 (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>), all the diffraction peaks are identified to be the standard peak of CoSn(OH)<small><sub>6</sub></small> (JCPDF card no. 13-0356), and no other impurities exist in the precursor. The SEM images (Fig. S2, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>) show that the precursor appears as 200â€“500 nm smooth cubes. Then, the cube precursor of CoSn(OH)<small><sub>6</sub></small> was annealed, respectively, at 400, 600, and 800 Â°C in an argon (Ar) atmosphere to synthesize a series of CSO samples with different degrees of crystallinity (see the Methods for detailed information) based on the decomposition reaction (<a title="" href="#eqn2">reaction (2)</a>). The inert atmosphere ensures that only temperature-induced physical changes take place during calcination, making crystallinity the principal variable undergoing modification. The chemical composition and phase structure of the material remain unchanged due to the absence of chemical reactions with external gases. The CSOs with different crystallinity were denoted as CSO-A, CSO-AC, and CSO-C, where A and C represent the amorphous and crystalline structures of CSO, respectively.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig1"><a href="/image/article/2024/NJ/d4nj02152e/d4nj02152e-f1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4nj02152e-f1.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NJ/d4nj02152e/d4nj02152e-f1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 1 </b> <span id="fig1"><span class="graphic_title">(a) Illustration of the fabrication process and schematic structure of the CSO samples at different crystallinity. (b) The XRD patterns of CSO samples (CSO-A, CSO-AC, and CSO-C). The high-resolution TEM images of (c) and (d) CSO-A and (e) and (f) CSO-AC at different resolutions (see the scale bar). (d) and (f) Are the enlarged images of red squares in (c) and (e), respectively.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">Coprecipitation reaction:</p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn1"><span id="eqn1">Sn<small><sup>4+</sup></small> + Co<small><sup>2+</sup></small>+ 60H<small><sup>âˆ’</sup></small> â†’ CoSn(OH)<small><sub>6</sub></small></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(1)</td></tr></tbody></table><p></p>
        <p class="otherpara">Decomposition reaction:</p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn2"><a href="/image/article/2024/NJ/d4nj02152e/d4nj02152e-t1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4nj02152e-t1.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NJ/d4nj02152e/d4nj02152e-t1.gif"></a></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(2)</td></tr></tbody></table><p></p>
        <p class="otherpara">Furthermore, XRD is employed to examine the crystal characteristics of the three CSO samples. The XRD results in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1b</a> show that CSO-A presents only a broad and weak peak, indicating a highly disordered amorphous structure. With an increase in annealing temperature, all the peaks of CSO-AC and CSO-C are indexed to the standard peaks of Co<small><sub>2</sub></small>SnO<small><sub>4</sub></small> (JCPDF card No. 29-0514) and SnO<small><sub>2</sub></small> (JCPDF card No. 41-1445), and no other phases are observed, indicating that the CSO samples are the mixture of SnO<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Co<small><sub>2</sub></small>SnO<small><sub>4</sub></small>. Compared with the XRD pattern of CSO-AC, the peak intensity increases, and the peak width becomes narrow in the XRD pattern of the CSO-C sample, implying higher crystallinity of CSO-C.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit20"><sup><span class="sup_ref">20</span></sup></a> The microstructure of CSO samples was also characterized by the high-resolution TEM images, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1câ€“f</a>. It could be seen that CSO-A presents a cubic morphology (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1c</a>), which is composed of amorphous domains (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1d</a>). With the increase of annealing temperature, CSO-AC displays an irregular morphology (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1e</a>), and the crystallinity of the sample was improved. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1f</a>, the lattice fringes observed in the HRTEM images of CSO-AC correspond to the (111) and (220) planes of Co<small><sub>2</sub></small>SnO<small><sub>4</sub></small>, in accord with the XRD data. Interestingly, some parts of amorphous domains (the area cycled by the red line in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1f</a>) also exist in the CSO-AC sample, forming an amorphousâ€“crystalline heterostructure. With a higher annealing temperature, the CSO-C sample presents higher crystallinity, and the HRTEM images (Fig. S3, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>) exhibit distinct lattice fringes without the amorphous structure.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">The detailed morphological characteristics of the three samples, including CSO-A, CSO-AC, and CSO-C, are measured by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) and the results are shown in Fig. S4 and S5 (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>). It could be observed that the original cubic morphology of the precursor remains in CSO-A (Fig. S4a, d and S5a, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>). However, as the annealing temperature increases, the morphology of CSO gradually evolves from uniform cubes to irregular aggregated nanoparticles, resulting in the breakdown of the cubic morphology and an increase in particle size. Fig. S5a (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>) shows that CSO-A exhibits a uniform cubic morphology composed of closely packed primary nanoparticles. With an increase of annealing temperature, CSO-AC displays nanoparticles in the range of 300â€“500 nm, composed of primary particles with sizes ranging from 20 to 60 nm (Fig. S4b, e and S5b, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>). Furthermore, a further increase in annealing temperature leads to the growth of both primary and secondary particle sizes for CSO-C (Fig. S4c, f, and S5c, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>) The nitrogen adsorption and desorption isotherms of CSO samples were carried out to acquire their specific surface area (SSA) and pore parameters. As shown in Fig. S6a (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>), the profiles of CSO-A exhibit combined I/II type isotherms with H3 type hysteresis loops, indicating the presence of both mesopores and macropores, being in accordance with the result of the pore size distribution (Fig. S6b, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>). However, with the increase of calcination temperature, CSO-AC and CSO-C show the characteristic type II feature with a relative H3-type hysteresis loop, indicating the existence of a macroporous structure.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit21"><sup><span class="sup_ref">21,22</span></sup></a> Calculated by the Brunauerâ€“Emmettâ€“Teller (BET) method, the SSA of CSO-A, CSO-AC, and CSO-C are, respectively, 59.8, 22.7, and 13.3 m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small>. The pore diameter of the maximum distribution calculated using the BJH method from the adsorption branch are, respectively, 3.7, 22.3, and 47. 6 nm. With an increase in the annealing temperature, the decrease of SSA and the increase of the pore size for CSO could be attributed to the nanoparticle aggregation, which is in accordance with the results of SEM images, as shown in Fig. S4 (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>).</p>
        <p class="otherpara">With an increase in annealing temperature, CSO-A, CSO-AC, and CSO-C samples show obvious color differences. The CSO-A sample is reed green, while the CSO-AC sample is black olive, and the CSO-C sample is ocean blue (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2a</a> and Fig. S7, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>). The color difference relates to the defects and electronic structure. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2b</a> and Fig. S8 (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>) compares the UV-vis spectra, which were then converted into the Tauc plots (see the Methods for more detailed information). The band gap can be estimated from the point of intersection of the baseline in the Tauc plot with a specific approximate line.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit23"><sup><span class="sup_ref">23,24</span></sup></a> The band gap of CSO-A, CSO-AC, and CSO-C is 0.75, 0.69, and 0.53 eV. The band gaps of CSO samples decreased with an increase in annealing temperature, indicating an increase in electronic conductivity. The Raman spectra of the CSO-A and CSO-AC samples were collected and are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2c</a>. Obviously, there are five peaks located at about 112.2, 171.8, 468.0, 510.8, and 664.6 cm<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small> in all the Raman spectra, corresponding to the vibration mode of the CSO. The peaks at 112.2 cm<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small> could be ascribed to the B<small><sub>1g</sub></small> Raman vibration mode of tinâ€“oxide (Snâ€“O) bonds.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit25"><sup><span class="sup_ref">25</span></sup></a> 171.8 and 510.8 cm<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small> are ascribed to the F<small><sub>2g</sub></small> Raman vibration mode of cobalt oxide (Coâ€“O) bonds.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit26"><sup><span class="sup_ref">26,27</span></sup></a> The peak at 468.0 cm<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small> is attributed to the E<small><sub>g</sub></small> Raman vibration mode of Coâ€“O and Snâ€“O; 664.6 cm<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small> is attributed to the A<small><sub>1g</sub></small> vibration mode of Coâ€“O and Snâ€“O. Compared to the Raman spectra of CSO-A, the five peaks in CSO-AC appear to have a tiny blue shift, which could be attributed to the size effects and oxygen vacancies.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit27"><sup><span class="sup_ref">27</span></sup></a> The Raman result indicates the three samples exhibit the same components but different microstructures. Furthermore, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2d</a>, CSO-A and CSO-AC exhibit a distinct signal at <span class="italic">g</span> = 2.003, usually ascribed to unpaired electrons in CSOs.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit28"><sup><span class="sup_ref">28</span></sup></a> The intensity of spectra is proportional to the concentration of unpaired electrons, indicating that the unpaired electron concentration in CSO-A is less than that in CSO-AC.</p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig2"><a href="/image/article/2024/NJ/d4nj02152e/d4nj02152e-f2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4nj02152e-f2.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NJ/d4nj02152e/d4nj02152e-f2.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 2 </b> <span id="fig2"><span class="graphic_title">The microstructure and electronic properties of CSO-A and CSO-AC samples. (a) The electronic photographs. (b) The Tauc plots of (<span class="italic">Î±hv</span>)<small><sup>2</sup></small> and <span class="italic">hv</span>; the inset shows the UV-vis DRS absorption spectra. (c) The Raman spectra. (d) EPR spectra. (e). The high-resolution O 1<span class="italic">s</span> XPS spectra. (f) The high-resolution Sn 3d XPS spectra.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was carried out to examine the elemental composition and valence states of CSO samples. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2e</a> and Fig. S9a (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>), the high-resolution O 1s XPS spectra of the CSO-A, CSO-AC, and CSO-C can be deconvoluted into three peaks. The peak at 530.4 eV relates to oxygen in the metalâ€“oxygen (Mâ€“O) bond. The peak at 531.4 eV is attributed to the oxygen vacancies, and the peak at 532.2 eV corresponds to the adsorbed water species (O<small><sub>H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O</sub></small>).<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit29"><sup><span class="sup_ref">29,30</span></sup></a> Based on the simulated results of O 1s XPS spectra of the CSOs, the oxygen vacancy content of CSO-A, CSO-AC, and CSO-C is about 31.0%, 13.1%, and 8.5%. Additionally, in the Sn 3d XPS spectra (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2f</a>), the strong peaks of the Sn 3d spectrum located at 486.0 and 494.4 eV can be assigned to Sn<small><sup>(4âˆ’<span class="italic">Î´</span>)+</sup></small> 3d<small><sub>5/2</sub></small> and Sn<small><sup>(4âˆ’<span class="italic">Î´</span>)+</sup></small> 3d<small><sub>3/2</sub></small>, respectively.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit31"><sup><span class="sup_ref">31</span></sup></a> However, the signals of the high-valence Sn<small><sup>4+</sup></small> in CSO-AC at 496.2 and 488.1 eV are increased, corresponding to the result of XRD and XPS O1s. The Co 2p spectra (Fig. S9b, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>) could be deconvoluted into two pairs of spinâ€“orbit doublets, <span class="italic">i.e.</span>, Co 2p<small><sub>3/2</sub></small> located at 780.9 eV and Co 2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small> located at 796.7 eV indicate the presence of Co<small><sup>2+</sup></small>, and the peaks located at 786.1 and 802.5 eV are attributed to their relative satellite peaks.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit32"><sup><span class="sup_ref">32</span></sup></a></p>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect461"><span class="b_heading">Theoretically calculation for the effect of crystallinity on the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> storage behaviors of CSO</span></h3>
        <span>Density functional theory (DFT) computations were conducted to deeply understand the impact of the crystallinity on the sodium ion storage behaviors of CSO. As shown in Fig. S10 (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>), we constructed three structure models with different degrees of crystallinity: CSO-A, CSO-AC, and CSO-C, corresponding to the amorphous, low crystalline, and high crystalline structures, respectively (see the Method for more detail). The electronic band structures of these three models are illustrated in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3aâ€“c</a>. It indicates that with the increase of the crystallinity, CSO-C shows a much smaller band gap of 0 eV in comparison with that of CSO-A (0.11 eV) and CSO-AC (0.06 eV), which indicates that the crystallinity changes the electron distribution of CSO. With the highest crystallinity, CSO-C exhibits the smallest band gap among the three samples, which means its highest electronic conductivity. These results are in accordance with the colour evolution of CSO (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2a</a> and Fig. S7, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>) and the results of UV-vis spectra (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2b</a> and Fig. S8, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>). Furthermore, to evaluate the effect of crystallinity on Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> storage behavior, the binding energies of Na ions (Î”<span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>b</sub></small>) in CSO-A, CSO-AC, and CSO-C were also calculated for comparison, according to the following equation:<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit33"><sup><span class="sup_ref">33</span></sup></a><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn3"><span id="eqn3">Î”<span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>b</sub></small> = <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>Na-CSO</sub></small> âˆ’ <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>CSO</sub></small> âˆ’ <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>Na</sub></small></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(3)</td></tr></tbody></table>Where <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>Na-CSO</sub></small>, <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>CSO</sub></small>, and <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>Na</sub></small> represent the energies of the Na inserted in CSO, CSO, and the Na atom, respectively. The structure models of CSO-A, CSO-AC, and CSO-C at the sodiation and desodiation states are shown in the inset of <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3d</a>. CSO-A with an amorphous structure exhibits the lowest binding energy (âˆ’3.65 eV), smaller than those of CSO-A (âˆ’2.58 eV) and CSO-C (âˆ’1.67 eV), suggesting CSO-A is the most energetically favorable for Na insertion.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig3"><a href="/image/article/2024/NJ/d4nj02152e/d4nj02152e-f3_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4nj02152e-f3.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NJ/d4nj02152e/d4nj02152e-f3.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 3 </b> <span id="fig3"><span class="graphic_title">The simulated band structure of (a) CSO-A, (b) CSO-AC, and (c) CSO-C. (d) The binding energy for sodium storage in the three calculated structure models; the inset image is the structure models of the three samples at the sodiation and desodiation states. The energy barriers of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> diffusion pathways of (e) CSO-A, (f) CSO-AC, and (g) CSO-C, and the insets show the diffusion path. In these models, the gray spheres represent the Sn atom; the blue is the cobalt atom, the red is the oxygen atom, and the yellow is the sodium atom.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">The energy barriers of ion diffusion pathways in different structural models were calculated. As depicted in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3eâ€“g</a>, CSO-A with an amorphous structure exhibits the lowest Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> diffusion barriers (0.41 eV), compared with the less crystalline CSO-A (0.77 eV) and high crystalline (1.35 eV) counterparts, indicating that Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> diffusion in the amorphous structure is faster than that in low and high crystalline materials. Above all, the crystallinity can affect the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> ion storage behaviors, and the amorphous structure of CSO-A will benefit Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> storage, accounting for its lowest binding energy with Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> and the lowest energy barrier for ion diffusion. Thus, CSO-A may deliver the highest specific capacitance.</p>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect503"><span class="b_heading">The Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> storage mechanism and electrochemical performance of CSO electrodes with different degrees of crystallinity</span></h3>
        <span>Generally, the main electrochemical process of CSO includes the conversion reaction of the oxide and the alloying reaction of Sn metal. To clarify and further illustrate the storage mechanism of cobaltâ€“tin oxide composites as anodes for sodium-ion batteries, we conducted <span class="italic">ex situ</span> XRD (Fig. S11, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>) and XPS (Fig. S12, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>) analyses on CSO-A at various charge and discharge states.</span>
        <p class="otherpara">As presented in Fig. S11 (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>), after the electrode was first discharged to 1.0 V, characteristic peaks corresponding to Sn and Co species were detected. When the discharge proceeded to 0.1 V and 0.01 V, the peaks attributed to Sn became more pronounced, alongside the emergence of Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Sn peaks, indicating the partial alloying reaction of sodium with tin. It is important to note that Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>O, a product of the conversion reaction, was not detected. This absence may be due to the amorphous nature of Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>O, which lacks associated Bragg peaks. Notably, after recharging the electrode to 1.0 V, the Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Sn peaks disappeared, and a new phase corresponding to SnO<small><sub>2</sub></small> was observed. Upon further charging to 3.0 V, weak peaks of both SnO<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Sn persisted, suggesting that the CSO-A in Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> half-cells undergoes incomplete oxidation, with a significant portion of the newly formed oxide remaining amorphous.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">Furthermore, <span class="italic">ex situ</span> XPS analysis was employed to monitor the transformation of Sn and Co during the electrochemical process. As shown in Fig. S12 (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>), the fully discharged state exhibited downshifted peaks corresponding to lower binding energies associated with Sn<small><sup>0</sup></small> and Co<small><sup>0</sup></small> states. This shift indicates the formation of Liâ€“Sn alloys and the presence of metallic Co. In contrast, the fully charged state displayed partially recoverable peaks relative to the pristine state, signifying the conversion of the metals back to their oxide forms.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">In summary, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4a</a>, during the first sodiation process, the cobaltâ€“tin oxide composite is converted to sodium oxide (Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>O), cobalt (Co), and tin (Sn) metal, and then alloyed with Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> to form Naâ€“Sn alloy (Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Sn, <span class="italic">x</span> â‰¤ 3.75) as expressed in <a title="" href="#eqn4">eqn (4) and (5)</a>.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit34"><sup><span class="sup_ref">34,35</span></sup></a></p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig4"><a href="/image/article/2024/NJ/d4nj02152e/d4nj02152e-f4_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4nj02152e-f4.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NJ/d4nj02152e/d4nj02152e-f4.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 4 </b> <span id="fig4"><span class="graphic_title">(a) Schematic diagram of the main electrochemical reaction of the tinâ€“cobalt oxide composite. (b) The cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of the CSO-A and CSO-AC electrodes for the second cycles at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small> in the voltage window between 0.01 and 3.0 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>. (c) The charge and discharge profiles of the CSO-A and CSO-AC electrodes for the second cycles at a current density of 50 mA g<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small>. (d) Cycling stability of the CSO-A and CSO-AC samples at a current density of 50 mA g<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small> for activated and 500 mA g<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small> for cycling. The charge curves of (e) CSO-A and (f) CSO-AC electrodes at the 5th, 10th, 20th, 50th, and 100th cycles at a current density of 500 mA g<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small>. (g) The reversible specific capacity of CSO-A and CSO-AC electrodes from the conversion reaction and alloying reaction process, separately, at the 5th, 10th, 20th, 50th, and 100th cycles. The data were collected from the charge curves in (e) and (f) at a current density of 500 mA g<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small>.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">Conversion reaction:</p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn4"><span id="eqn4">Co<small><sub>2</sub></small>SnO<small><sub>4</sub></small> + SnO<small><sub>2</sub></small> + 2<span class="italic">z</span>Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> + 2<span class="italic">z</span>e<small><sup>âˆ’</sup></small> â†’ <span class="italic">x</span>Co + <span class="italic">y</span>Sn + <span class="italic">z</span>Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>Oâ€ƒ0.6â€“3.0 V</span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(4)</td></tr></tbody></table><p></p>
        <p class="otherpara">Alloying reaction:</p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn5"><span id="eqn5">Sn + <span class="italic">x</span>Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> +<span class="italic">x</span>e<small><sup>âˆ’</sup></small> â†” Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Snâ€ƒ 0.01â€“0.6 V</span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(5)</td></tr></tbody></table><p></p>
        <p class="otherpara">During the first desodiation process, Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Sn is gradually de-alloyed to Sn metal, and then the Sn and Co metals are, respectively, oxidized to tin oxide (SnO<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>, 0 &lt; <span class="italic">x</span> â‰¤ 2) and cobalt oxide (CoO<small><sub><span class="italic">y</span></sub></small>, 0 &lt; <span class="italic">y</span> â‰¤ 1), as shown in <a title="" href="#eqn6">eqn (6)â€“(8)</a>.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">Alloying reaction:</p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn6"><span id="eqn6">Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Sn â†” Sn + <span class="italic">x</span>Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> + <span class="italic">x</span>e<small><sup>âˆ’</sup></small>â€ƒ 0.01â€“1.0 V</span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(6)</td></tr></tbody></table><p></p>
        <p class="otherpara">Conversion reaction:</p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn7"><span id="eqn7">Sn + <span class="italic">x</span>Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>O â†” SnO<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small> + 2<span class="italic">x</span>Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> + 2<span class="italic">x</span>e<small><sup>âˆ’</sup></small>â€ƒ1 0â€“3.0 V</span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(7)</td></tr></tbody></table><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn8"><span id="eqn8">Co + <span class="italic">y</span>Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>O â†” CoO<small><sub><span class="italic">y</span></sub></small> + 2<span class="italic">y</span>Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> + 2<span class="italic">y</span>e<small><sup>âˆ’</sup></small>â€ƒ1.0â€“3.0 V</span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(8)</td></tr></tbody></table><p></p>
        <p class="otherpara">After the first cycle, the following sodiation and desodiation processes will be based on the reversible conversion and alloying reaction, as shown in <a title="" href="#eqn6">eqn (6)â€“(8)</a>. Due to the slow ion and electron kinetics of CSO, all the species that participated in the above reaction will exist simultaneously.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">To investigate the electrochemical properties and relevant Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> storage mechanism, cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurements of the CSO-A, CSO-AC, and CSO-C electrodes were performed. As shown in Fig. S13aâ€“c (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>), the CV curves of the three samples are quite distinct, indicating the obvious effect of crystallinity on the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> storage behaviors. The CSO-A electrode exhibits an intense and broad cathodic peak at about 0.32 V during the first sodiation process (Fig. S13a, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>), which is related to the formation of a solidâ€“electrolyte interphase (SEI) along the electrode surface and initiation of the conversion reaction of CSO.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit6"><sup><span class="sup_ref">6</span></sup></a> The formation of a Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Sn alloy is observed at the end of the cathodic scan near 0.01 V. During the desodiation process, there are two distinct peaks, at 0.12 and 1.27 V, respectively, related to the dealloying and conversion reactions. From the second cycle, two pairs of reduction/oxidation peaks, localized at 0.01 V/0.12 V and 0.54 V/1.27 V, remained stable, corresponding to the alloying/dealloying reaction and reversible conversion reaction.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit36"><sup><span class="sup_ref">36</span></sup></a> However, during the first sodiation process, the CSO-AC (Fig. S13b, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>) and CSO-C (Fig. S13c, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>) electrodes show three peaks at about 2 V, 1 V, 0.3 V, and 0.01 V, attributed to the conversion and alloying reaction of CSO. At the second and third sodiation processes, the peaks above 1.0 V disappear. Only the peaks at 0.59 V and 0.01 V remain. During the first three cycles of CV curves for the desodiation process, the CSO-AC and CSO-C electrodes present three numbers of anodic peaks, but the peak position and intensity of the two electrodes are distinct, indicating the effect of crystallinity on the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> storage behaviors.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">Furthermore, the galvanostatic chargeâ€“discharge profiles of CSO electrodes were collected, as shown in Fig. S13dâ€“f (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>). The CSO-A (Fig. S13d, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>) shows a characterized sloping discharge profile due to a highly porous and disordered structure but exhibits the highest reversible specific capacity of 408 mA h g<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small> and highest initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 67.5% at a current density of 50 mA g<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small>. However, it is important to note that the specific capacity of CSO-A decreases with subsequent cycles, indicating some degree of capacity fade. The discharging profiles of the CSO-AC (Fig. S13e, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>) and CSO-C (Fig. S13f, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>) show an obvious plateau region below 0.2 V but a lower reversible specific capacity of 289 and 168 mA h g<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small>, and inferior ICE of 38.0% and 43.1%. Despite their lower ICE values, these samples maintain a more stable specific capacity over the following cycles, suggesting better capacity retention. Above all, all of the discharge profiles of CSO are composed of a slope region (above 1.0 V) and a plateau region (between 1.0 V and 0.01 V), respectively, attributed to the conversion reaction and alloying reaction of CSO. The corresponding differential capacity plots (d<span class="italic">Q</span>/d<span class="italic">V</span>) of CSO, obtained from charge/discharge profiles in Fig. S13dâ€“f (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>), are depicted in Fig. S14aâ€“c (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>). The peak intensity and position of all three samples are similar to the results of corresponding CV curves, indicating the effect of crystallinity on the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> storage behaviors.</p>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect640"><span class="b_heading">Quantitatively investigating the effect of crystallinity on Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> storage behaviors</span></h3>
        <span>The CV curves of the two samples at a sweep rate of 0.1 mV s<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small> were compared in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4b</a>. It can be observed that the CSO-A electrode exhibits a pair of broad and high current redox peaks at 0.54 and 1.28 V, attributed to the reversible conversion reaction of cobaltâ€“tin oxide composites. The larger current of the redox peak suggests a higher specific capacity. However, with the increase of crystallinity, the current of this broad redox peak decreases in CSO-AC and CSO-C, implying that the decrease in the specific capacity originated from the conversion reaction. In addition, a distinct sharp peak at 0.11 V appears in CSO-C, indicating that the alloying reaction plays an important role in the crystalline sample. Furthermore, compared to the charge profile of CSO-A and CSO-AC electrodes (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4c</a>), CSO-A with amorphous structure exhibits the highest reversible specific capacity and the lowest polarization, indicating the lowest binding energy of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> and the lowest energy barrier for Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> diffusion, which is consistent with the theoretical calculation results (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3</a>). The cycling performance of the three CSO electrodes at low and high current densities of 500 and 1000 mA g<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small> is presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4d</a> and Fig. S15 (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>). Although the CSO-A presents the highest initial reversible capacity, during cycling, the reversible specific capacity will be decreased lower than that of CSO-AC up to 30 cycles. The CSO-AC electrode delivers a relatively lower initial specific capacity but presents the best cycling stability and highest reversible charge capacity after cycling.</span>
        <p class="otherpara">Furthermore, to reveal the correlation between crystalline and specific capacity, we divided the charge profiles into two segments according to the evolution trend of CV and d<span class="italic">Q</span>/d<span class="italic">V</span> curves at different voltage regions. Specifically, in the charge profiles of CSO (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4e and f</a>, and Fig. S16a, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>), the reversible specific capacity above 1.0 V could be attributed to the conversion reaction and the reversible specific capacity below 1.0 V results from the alloying reaction. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4g</a> and Fig. S16b (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>), the charge capacity contributions of three CSO samples from the conversion and alloying reaction based on charge capacity curves at different cycles were compared. It is observed that the charge capacities gradually decreased at 0.01â€“1.0 V for the CSO-A electrode, but the charge capacities at 1.0â€“3.0 V decay quickly from 196.1 mA h g<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small> to 96.7 mA h g<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small> after 100 cycles. However, for the CSO-AC and CSO-C electrodes, the charge capacities remained virtually unchanged in the potential region of 0.01â€“1.0 V and 1.0â€“3.0 V, indicating their good stability for the reversible reformation of cobaltâ€“tin oxide composites during cycling. The specific capacity of the conversion reaction rapidly decreases for CSO-A, but it remains stable for CSO-AC and CSO-C, demonstrated in Fig. S17 (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>) during the 1000 cycling at a high current density of 1000 mA h g<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small>. Although the CSO-A electrode owns the highest initial specific capacity, the capacity retention of the conversion reaction for CSO-AC (89.5%) is much higher than that of CSO-A (49.3%), indicating a highly reversible conversion reaction at the CSO-AC electrode, which corresponds to the unchanged peak at 1.28 V after cycling in the d<span class="italic">Q</span>/d<span class="italic">V</span> plots (Fig. S18, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>).</p>
        <p class="otherpara">To gain a deep insight into the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> storage behaviors and quantify the effect of the crystallinity degree on Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> storage, CV curves of CSOs were measured at various scan rates from 0.2 to 2.0 mV s<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small> using Na metal as an anode. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5a and b</a>, and Fig. S19a (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>), the cathodic/anodic currents increase with the scan rates. Generally, the correlation between current (<span class="italic">i</span>) and the scan rate (<span class="italic">v</span>) in a CV curve can be used to distinguish the charge storage process based on <a title="" href="#eqn9">eqn (9)</a>:<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit37"><sup><span class="sup_ref">37,38</span></sup></a></p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn9"><span id="eqn9"><span class="italic">i</span> = <span class="italic">av</span><small><sup><span class="italic">b</span></sup></small></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(9)</td></tr></tbody></table>where <span class="italic">a</span> and <span class="italic">b</span> are adjustable constants, typically; when <span class="italic">b</span> is equal to 0.5, <span class="italic">i</span> is proportional to the square root of <span class="italic">v</span>, indicating a traditional diffusion-controlled charge-storage process. While <span class="italic">b</span> = 1.0, <span class="italic">i</span> is linearly proportional to <span class="italic">v</span>, meaning a capacitive-dominated charge-storage process. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5c</a> and Fig. S19b (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>), it is worth noting that the <span class="italic">b</span> value of CSO-A (0.89) is significantly lower than those of the other two samples (CSO-AC: 1.02, CSO-C: 1.07). Furthermore, the diffusion and capacitive contributions to Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> storage can be quantitatively separated according to <a title="" href="#eqn10">eqn (10)</a><a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit39"><sup><span class="sup_ref">39,40</span></sup></a><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn10"><span id="eqn10"><span class="italic">i</span>(<span class="italic">V</span>) = <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>1</sub></small><span class="italic">v</span> + <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>2</sub></small><span class="italic">v</span><small><sup>1/2</sup></small></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(10)</td></tr></tbody></table>where <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>1</sub></small> and <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>2</sub></small> are the fitting constants, and <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>1</sub></small><span class="italic">v</span> and <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>2</sub></small><span class="italic">v</span><small><sup>1/2</sup></small> represent, respectively, the capacitive and diffusion contributions at the measured current density. At various scan rates, the pseudocapacitive contribution of CSO-A is lower than those of the other two samples (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5d</a> and Fig. S19c, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>). For example, at a large scan rate of 2.0 mV s<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small>, the pseudocapacitive contribution of CSO-A is 75.1%, while those of CSO-AC and CSO-C are 88.9% and 90.2%, respectively (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5e</a> and Fig. S19c, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>), suggesting the crystal structure will improve the pseudocapacitance contribution, the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> storage for CSO-A with a highly disordered structure is a diffusion-controlled process, while the CSO-AC and CSO-C samples with crystalline structures are a capacitive-dominated charge-storage process.<p></p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig5"><a href="/image/article/2024/NJ/d4nj02152e/d4nj02152e-f5_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4nj02152e-f5.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NJ/d4nj02152e/d4nj02152e-f5.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 5 </b> <span id="fig5"><span class="graphic_title">CV curves of (a) CSO-A and (b) CSO-AC electrodes at different scan rates from 0.2 to 2.0 mV s<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small>. (c) The relationship between the logarithm cathodic peak current and logarithm scan rates at a voltage of 1.50 V. (d) The percentage of the capacitance contribution for CSO-A and CSO-AC electrodes at different scan rates. (e) CV curve of CSO-AC at a sweep rate of 2.0 mV s<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small>, the estimated capacitive current contribution is shown in the shaded region. (f) The Nyquist plots of the CSO-A and CSO-AC electrodes after 1000 cycles at a current density of 1000 mA g<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small>, the inset shows <span class="italic">Z</span>â€² as a function of the <span class="italic">Ï‰</span><small><sup>âˆ’1/2</sup></small> plot in the low-frequency range, and the slope of fitting curves is the Warburg factor, <span class="italic">Ïƒ</span><small><sub>w</sub></small>.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">Additionally, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) after cycling was conducted to investigate the conductivity enhancement and helped to further understand the reasons for the distinct electrochemical performances of CSO electrodes. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5f</a>, the EIS spectra consist of two parts: a semi-circle in the high-frequency region related to the charge transfer resistance (<span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small>) of the electrodes and a sloping line in the low-frequency region named the Warburg region that affects the diffusion of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> ions in the electrode bulk, and the crosspoint of EIS curve and <span class="italic">Z</span>â€² axis is the ohmic resistance (<span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>s</sub></small>) of the electrode. CSO-AC presents a smaller semi-circle, indicating that the <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small> value of the CSO-AC sample (46.9 Î©) is smaller than that of the CSO-A sample (122.3 Î©), and the <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>s</sub></small> value of the CSO-AC sample (56.9 Î©) is smaller than that of CSO-A sample (122.5 Î©). These results indicate the significantly enhanced conductivity in the CSO-AC sample. In addition, the sodium ion diffusion coefficient, <span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Na+</sub></small>, can be calculated from the Warburg region (see Methods for a detailed calculation process). The Warburg factor could be estimated from the slope of the inset image in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5f</a>. The <span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Na<small><sup>+</sup></small></sub></small> of the CSO-A electrode is 4.73 Ã— 10<small><sup>âˆ’15</sup></small> cm<small><sup>2</sup></small> s<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small>, which is over twice higher than that of the CSO-AC electrode (2.13 Ã— 10<small><sup>âˆ’15</sup></small> cm<small><sup>2</sup></small> s<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small>), demonstrating the diffusion-controlled process of CSO-A.</p>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect761"><span class="b_heading">Correlation between the crystallinity, microstructure, and the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> storage mechanism</span></h3>
        <span>All the experimental and theoretical results related to structure and electrochemical properties are presented in Table S1 and Table S2 (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>), and in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6</a>. This study strongly demonstrates that the highly porous and disordered structure in CSO-A could increase the concentration of oxygen vacancy, lower the binding energy with Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>, lower the energy barrier for Na ion diffusion, and promote the occurrence of the conversion reaction, resulting in the highest initial specific capacity of CSO-A and the highest Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> diffusion coefficient. With the increase of annealing temperature, the percent of ordered structure increases, the band gap decreases, and the electronic conductivity increases, resulting in the improved reversibility of the conversion reaction and the increase of pseudocapacitance contribution for CSO-AC and CSO-C. Although higher crystallinity and intact nanostructure are beneficial for the stability of the electrode, the initial specific capacity is lower. Thus, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6b</a>, the CSO-AC sample, with amorphous and crystal domains and a lower band gap, presents the best electrochemical performance, originating from its high percent of pseudocapacitive contribution, high reversibility of the conversion reaction, and low charge transfer resistance.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig6"><a href="/image/article/2024/NJ/d4nj02152e/d4nj02152e-f6_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4nj02152e-f6.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NJ/d4nj02152e/d4nj02152e-f6.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 6 </b> <span id="fig6"><span class="graphic_title">(a) Schematic illustration of the evolution of crystallinity for CSO-A, CSO-AC, and CSO-C samples and their optimal structureâ€“activity relationship. (b) Radar chart that correlated the structure and electrochemical properties of the three CSO samples for Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> storage. All the data are summarized in Table S1 (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">â€ </a>). The shaded area represents the comprehensive performance of CSO-A, CSO-AC, and CSO-C.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      
    
    
      <h2 id="sect774"><span class="a_heading">Conclusions</span></h2>
      <span>In summary, among the three CSO samples with varying degrees of crystallinity, CSO-A delivers the highest initial reversible specific capacity of 408 mA h g<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small> at a current density of 50 mA g<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small>, accounting for its high specific surface area, highly porous and disordered structure, and high concentration of oxygen vacancy. Additionally, theoretical calculation proved that CSO-A presents the lowest binding energy (âˆ’3.65 eV) and Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> diffusion barriers (0.41 eV), indicating the most energetically favorable for Na insertion. However, CSO-A suffers from rapid capacity fading, leading to the reversible specific capacity above 30 cycles lower than that of CSO-AC with a lower initial reversible specific capacity. Although the crystallinity, microstructure, electronic conductivity, and physicochemical properties of CSO-AC are located at the middle position among the three samples, CSO-AC shows the highest capacity retention and reversible specific capacity after cycling. Quantitative analysis demonstrated that the appreciated crystallinity improves the reversibility of the conversion reaction and enhances the pseudocapacitance contribution; the rational disordered structure provides more active sites to improve the initial specific capacity. Thus, for alloy anodes, part of crystallinity is beneficial for improving capacity retention.</span>
    
    
      <h2 id="sect780"><span class="a_heading">Materials and methods</span></h2>
      <span>All the reagents were of analytical grade purity and used without any further purification.</span>
      
        <h3 id="sect783"><span class="b_heading">The synthesis of cobaltâ€“tin oxide composites (CSOs)</span></h3>
        <span>The precursor, cobalt hydroxystannates (CoSn(OH)<small><sub>6</sub></small>), was fabricated by mixing 15 mL of 0.2 M tin(<span class="small_caps">IV</span>) chloride pentahydrate (SnCl<small><sub>4</sub></small>Â·5H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O) solution and 15 mL of 0.2 M cobalt(<span class="small_caps">II</span>) nitrate hexahydrate (Co(NO<small><sub>3</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>Â·6H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O) solution at room temperature for 20 min. Then, 3 mmol sodium citrate (C<small><sub>6</sub></small>H<small><sub>5</sub></small>Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>O<small><sub>7</sub></small>Â·2H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O) was added into the above solution under stirring. Finally, the 30 mL of 4 M sodium hydroxide (NaOH) solution was added drop by drop into the above solution. After stirring for 1 h, the pink precipitate was collected and washed with distilled water and ethanol to remove residual ions. Then, the product was dried overnight in a vacuum oven at 100 Â°C. Finally, three cobaltâ€“tin oxide composite samples, named CSO-A, CSO-AC, and CSO-C, were respectively synthesized by annealing the precursor (CoSn(OH)<small><sub>6</sub></small>) at various calcination temperatures such as 400, 600, and 800 Â°C for 2 h in an argon (Ar) atmosphere with a slow ramp rate of 2 Â°C min<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small>.</span>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect801"><span class="b_heading">Material characterization</span></h3>
        <span>An X-ray diffractometer (D8 Advance diffractometer, Bruker, Germany) with Cu KÎ± radiation (<span class="italic">V</span> = 40 kV, <span class="italic">I</span> = 40 mA, and <span class="italic">Î»</span> = 1.5418 Ã…) was used to acquire X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns and confirm the phase of samples. A Raman spectrometer (LabRAM HR Evolution) with an excitation source of 532 nm was applied to obtain the microstructure of samples. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) measurements were performed on an X-ray photoelectron spectrometer (Thermo Fisher ESCALAB 250Xi system) at room temperature with mono Al KÎ± radiation to characterize the valence information of Co, Sn, and O. Room-temperature electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) tests were carried out on an EPR spectrometer (A300-10-12, Bruker). The ultraviolet-visible (UV-Vis) absorption spectra were recorded using PerkinElmer Lambda 950 spectrometers with an integrating sphere. The UV-sis spectra were converted into Tauc plots using the following equation:<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit23"><sup><span class="sup_ref">23,24</span></sup></a><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn11"><span id="eqn11">(<span class="italic">Î±hv</span>)<small><sup>1/<span class="italic">n</span></sup></small> = <span class="italic">A</span>(<span class="italic">hv</span> âˆ’ <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>g</sub></small>)</span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(11)</td></tr></tbody></table>where <span class="italic">A</span> is a constant, <span class="italic">hv</span> is photon energy, <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>g</sub></small> is the band gap, and <span class="italic">n</span> is 1/2 for direct transition and 2 for indirect transition. The absorption coefficient <span class="italic">Î±</span> can be obtained using the following relation:<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit23"><sup><span class="sup_ref">23</span></sup></a><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn12"><a href="/image/article/2024/NJ/d4nj02152e/d4nj02152e-t2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4nj02152e-t2.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NJ/d4nj02152e/d4nj02152e-t2.gif"></a></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(12)</td></tr></tbody></table>where <span class="italic">d</span> is the thickness of the film and <span class="italic">T</span> is the transmittance measured by UV-vis spectroscopy.</span>
        <p class="otherpara">Transmission electron microscopy (TEM, JEOL 3200F, Japan) was used to characterize the morphology and microstructures. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images were obtained from a scanning electron microscope (JEOL JSM-6380LV FE-SEM) to observe the morphological evolution of CSO. The nitrogen adsorption and desorption isotherms were obtained using a Micromeritics ASAP 2420-4 volumetric adsorption analyzer (USA) at 77 K. The specific surface area (SSA) and pore size distribution were calculated, respectively, using the BET theory and BJH method.</p>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect831"><span class="b_heading">Electrochemical measurements</span></h3>
        <span>For the preparation of working electrodes, typically, active material (CSO-A, CSO-AC, and CSO-C), acetylene black, and sodium carboxymethyl cellulose (CMC) were mixed based on a mass ratio of 8<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1 in deionized water to form a homogeneous slurry.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit41"><sup><span class="sup_ref">41</span></sup></a> The slurry was coated on a copper foil by the doctor blade method, and then dried in a vacuum oven at 90 Â°C for 12 h. Finally, the foil covered with active materials was punched into some discs with a diameter of 12 mm as a working electrode. The mass loading of the active materials is about 1.25 mg cm<small><sup>âˆ’2</sup></small>. CR2032 coin-type cells were assembled in an argon (Ar) filled glove box (Mikrouna, H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O &lt; 0.1 ppm, O<small><sub>2</sub></small> &lt; 0.1 ppm). In the cells, sodium metal is used as the counter and reference electrodes. Glass fiber membranes (Whatman, GF/D) and 1 M NaPF<small><sub>6</sub></small> in a mixture of diethyl carbonate (DEC) and ethylene carbonate (EC) with a volume ratio of 1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1 were used as the separator and electrolyte, respectively. Before testing, the new cells were rested overnight. A Neware CT-4008T instrument measured the galvanostatic charge and discharge properties at different current densities in the 0.01â€“3.00 V voltage range <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>. CV measurements were carried out using a Bio-Logic electrochemistry workstation at different scan rates in the voltage range of 0.01â€“3.00 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>.</span>
        <p class="otherpara">The sodium ion diffusion coefficient, <span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Na+</sub></small>, can be calculated from the Warburg region using <a title="" href="#eqn13">eqn (13)</a>:<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit42"><sup><span class="sup_ref">42</span></sup></a></p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn13"><a href="/image/article/2024/NJ/d4nj02152e/d4nj02152e-t3_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4nj02152e-t3.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NJ/d4nj02152e/d4nj02152e-t3.gif"></a></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(13)</td></tr></tbody></table>where <span class="italic">R</span> is the universal gas constant, <span class="italic">T</span> is the absolute temperature, <span class="italic">A</span> is the electrode area of the electrode, <span class="italic">n</span> is the number of electrons, <span class="italic">F</span> is the Faraday constant and <span class="italic">C</span> is the concentration of sodium ions in the electrolyte (10<small><sup>âˆ’3</sup></small> mol cm<small><sup>âˆ’3</sup></small>). <span class="italic">Ïƒ</span><small><sub>w</sub></small> is the Warburg factor and <span class="italic">Ï‰</span> is the angular frequency according to <a title="" href="#eqn14">eqn (14)</a>. <span class="italic">Ïƒ</span><small><sub>w</sub></small> is related to <span class="italic">Z</span>â€² and can be obtained from the slope of the fitting line of the EIS data at low frequencies:<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit43"><sup><span class="sup_ref">43</span></sup></a><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn14"><a href="/image/article/2024/NJ/d4nj02152e/d4nj02152e-t4_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4nj02152e-t4.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NJ/d4nj02152e/d4nj02152e-t4.gif"></a></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(14)</td></tr></tbody></table><p></p>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect870"><span class="b_heading">Theoretical calculation methods</span></h3>
        <span>First principles calculations were performed using the Vienna <span class="italic">Ab initio</span> Simulation Package (VASP)<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit44"><sup><span class="sup_ref">44</span></sup></a> with the projector augmented wave (PAW) method.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit45"><sup><span class="sup_ref">45</span></sup></a> The exchange-functional is obtained using the Perdewâ€“Burkeâ€“Ernzerhof (PBE) functional,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit46"><sup><span class="sup_ref">46</span></sup></a> in combination with the DFT-D correction.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit47"><sup><span class="sup_ref">47</span></sup></a> The calculations were performed in a spin-polarized manner. The cut-off energy of the plane-wave basis is set at 450 eV. For the optimization of both geometry and lattice size, the Brillouin zone integration is performed with 2 Ã— 2 Ã— 2 Monkhorstâ€“Pack <span class="italic">k</span>-point sampling. The self-consistent calculations apply a convergence energy threshold of 10<small><sup>âˆ’5</sup></small> eV. The equilibrium geometries and lattice constants are optimized with maximum stress on each atom within 0.02 eV Ã…<small><sup>âˆ’1</sup></small>. The Na ion migration barriers in the three models' diffusion energy barriers were obtained by the climbing image nudged elastic band (NIB) method.</span>
        <p class="otherpara">The adsorption (<span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ad</sub></small>) was calculated using the following expression.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit48"><sup><span class="sup_ref">48</span></sup></a></p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn15"><span id="eqn15"><span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ad</sub></small> = <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>slab/Ads</sub></small> âˆ’ <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>slab</sub></small> âˆ’ <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>Ads</sub></small></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(15)</td></tr></tbody></table>where <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>slab/Ads</sub></small>, <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>slab</sub></small> and <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>Ads</sub></small> are, respectively, the total energies of adsorbates on adsorbents, isolated adsorbents, and adsorbates.<p></p>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect902"><span class="b_heading">The construction of models</span></h3>
        <span>The model with an amorphous structure, CSO-A, was established using Atomsk (https://atomsk.univ-lille.fr/) by the packing method. The packing density of atoms in the CSO-A model was the same as the model with a high crystallinity structure (CSO-C). The model with a low crystallinity structure (CSO-AC) was constructed by quenching CSO-C at 1500 K in a large-scale atomic/molecular massively parallel simulator (LAMMPS) in an NVT ensemble. The time step was 2.0 fs, and the total time was 100 ps. The final CSO-A, CSO-AC, and CSO-C models were further optimized in VASP.</span>

            <br><a href="https://cookiepedia.co.uk/giving-consent-to-cookies" class="privacy-notice-link" rel="noopener" target="_blank" aria-label="More information about your privacy, opens in a new tab">More information</a></div><button id="accept-recommended-btn-handler">Allow all</button><section class="ot-sdk-row ot-cat-grp"><h3 id="ot-category-title"> Manage consent preferences</h3><div class="ot-pli-hdr ot-leg-border-color"><span class="ot-li-title">Consent</span> <span class="ot-li-title">Legitimate interest</span></div><div class="ot-accordion-layout ot-cat-item ot-vs-config" data-optanongroupid="C0001"><button aria-expanded="false" ot-accordion="true" aria-controls="ot-desc-id-C0001" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0001 ot-status-id-C0001"></button><!-- Accordion header --><div class="ot-acc-hdr"><div class="ot-plus-minus"><span></span><span></span></div><h4 class="ot-cat-header" id="ot-header-id-C0001">Strictly necessary cookies</h4></div><!-- accordion detail --><div class="ot-acc-grpcntr ot-acc-txt"><p class="ot-acc-grpdesc ot-category-desc" id="ot-desc-id-C0001">These cookies are necessary for the website to function and cannot be switched off in our systems. They are usually only set in response to actions made by you which amount to a request for services, such as setting your privacy preferences, logging in or filling in forms. You can set your browser to block or alert you about these cookies, but some parts of the site will not then work. These cookies do not store any personally identifiable information.</p></div></div><div class="ot-accordion-layout ot-cat-item ot-vs-config" data-optanongroupid="C0002"><button aria-expanded="false" ot-accordion="true" aria-controls="ot-desc-id-C0002" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0002"></button><!-- Accordion header --><div class="ot-acc-hdr"><div class="ot-plus-minus"><span></span><span></span></div><h4 class="ot-cat-header" id="ot-header-id-C0002">Analytical cookies</h4><div class="ot-tgl"><input type="checkbox" name="ot-group-id-C0002" id="ot-group-id-C0002" role="switch" class="category-switch-handler" data-optanongroupid="C0002" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0002"> <label class="ot-switch" for="ot-group-id-C0002"><span class="ot-switch-nob"></span> <span class="ot-label-txt">Analytical cookies</span></label> </div></div><!-- accordion detail --><div class="ot-acc-grpcntr ot-acc-txt"><p class="ot-acc-grpdesc ot-category-desc" id="ot-desc-id-C0002">These cookies allow us to count visits and traffic sources so we can measure and improve the performance of our site. They help us to know which pages are the most and least popular and see how visitors move around the site. All information these cookies collect is aggregated and therefore anonymous. If you do not allow these cookies we will not know when you have visited our site, and will not be able to monitor its performance.</p></div></div><div class="ot-accordion-layout ot-cat-item ot-vs-config" data-optanongroupid="C0003"><button aria-expanded="false" ot-accordion="true" aria-controls="ot-desc-id-C0003" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0003"></button><!-- Accordion header --><div class="ot-acc-hdr"><div class="ot-plus-minus"><span></span><span></span></div><h4 class="ot-cat-header" id="ot-header-id-C0003">Functional cookies</h4><div class="ot-tgl"><input type="checkbox" name="ot-group-id-C0003" id="ot-group-id-C0003" role="switch" class="category-switch-handler" data-optanongroupid="C0003" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0003"> <label class="ot-switch" for="ot-group-id-C0003"><span class="ot-switch-nob"></span> <span class="ot-label-txt">Functional cookies</span></label> </div></div><!-- accordion detail --><div class="ot-acc-grpcntr ot-acc-txt"><p class="ot-acc-grpdesc ot-category-desc" id="ot-desc-id-C0003">These cookies enable the website to provide enhanced functionality and personalisation. They may be set by us or by third party providers whose services we have added to our pages. If you do not allow these cookies then some or all of these services may not function properly.</p></div></div><div class="ot-accordion-layout ot-cat-item ot-vs-config" data-optanongroupid="C0004"><button aria-expanded="false" ot-accordion="true" aria-controls="ot-desc-id-C0004" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0004"></button><!-- Accordion header --><div class="ot-acc-hdr"><div class="ot-plus-minus"><span></span><span></span></div><h4 class="ot-cat-header" id="ot-header-id-C0004">Targeting cookies</h4><div class="ot-tgl"><input type="checkbox" name="ot-group-id-C0004" id="ot-group-id-C0004" role="switch" class="category-switch-handler" data-optanongroupid="C0004" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0004"> <label class="ot-switch" for="ot-group-id-C0004"><span class="ot-switch-nob"></span> <span class="ot-label-txt">Targeting cookies</span></label> </div></div><!-- accordion detail --><div class="ot-acc-grpcntr ot-acc-txt"><p class="ot-acc-grpdesc ot-category-desc" id="ot-desc-id-C0004">These cookies may be set through our site by our advertising partners.
